(2) Exactly k of the n lines are sunny.

Definition: A line is sunny if it is not parallel to the x-axis, the y-axis, or the line x+y=0.

Thus a line is not sunny if it is parallel to x-axis (horizontal), parallel to y-axis (vertical), or parallel to x+y=0 (i.e., slope -1). So sunny lines are those with slopes not equal to 0, undefined (vertical), or -1. Also note: the line x+y=0 has slope -1. So sunny lines have slopes s ∈ ℝ \ {0, ∞, -1}. But the problem says "not parallel to any of the x-axis, the y-axis, or the line x+y=0". So indeed any line that is horizontal, vertical, or slope -1 is not sunny. All other lines are sunny.

We have n distinct lines, exactly k sunny. So n - k lines are not sunny, i.e., they are either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. But note: distinct lines, so they could be multiple horizontals, multiples verticals, multiples slope -1, but they are all distinct lines.

Condition (1): For all positive integers a, b with a+b ≤ n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines. So we have a finite set of integer lattice points: all points (a,b) with a,b positive integers (positive meaning ≥1) and a+b ≤ n+1. That is a triangular region of points. We need to cover all these points with n lines (distinct). The lines can be any lines in the plane, not necessarily passing through integer points. But they must contain each such point.

We are to determine all possible k (nonnegative integers) for which such a set of lines exists.

We need to find constraints on k given n, and then show that all k satisfying some condition are attainable.

Let's denote S = {(a,b) ∈ ℤ^2 : a≥1, b≥1, a+b ≤ n+1}. Number of points: For each sum s from 2 to n+1, there are s-1 points (since a from 1 to s-1, b = s-a). So total points = Σ_{s=2}^{n+1} (s-1) = Σ_{t=1}^{n} t = n(n+1)/2. That's the number of points.

We have n lines covering these points. Each line can cover many points, but note that points are on integer lattice. However lines can be arbitrary, not necessarily through integer points; but they must pass through each point in S. So each point must lie on some line. The lines are distinct.

We need to find possible k such that there exists a set of n lines with exactly k sunny lines covering all points.

We need to consider the covering properties. This is reminiscent of covering a set of points with lines, like the "no three collinear" or "covering with lines" problems. Here we have many points, and we need exactly n lines. Since there are n(n+1)/2 points, the average number of points per line is (n+1)/2. That is not huge; lines can cover many points if they contain many collinear points.

So we have exactly three pairs that yield sunny lines: { (1,1),(2,2) } with slope1, { (1,2),(3,1) } with slope -1/2, { (1,3),(2,1) } with slope -2. These three lines are distinct: line1: y=x? Actually (1,1) and (2,2) gives y=x, slope1, sunny. line2: through (1,2) and (3,1): equation y-2 = (-1/2)(x-1) => y = -1/2 x + 2.5, sunny. line3: through (1,3) and (2,1): slope (1-3)/(2-1) = -2, equation y-3 = -2(x-1) => y = -2x+5, sunny. So these three lines cover all points: line1 covers (1,1) and (2,2); line2 covers (1,2) and (3,1); line3 covers (1,3) and (2,1). All 6 points covered. Exactly 3 sunny lines. So k=3 works.

Thus for n=3, possible k: 0,1,3. k=2 not possible.

Now n=4. Let's test small n to see pattern. n≥3, so n=4. Points: a,b positive, a+b ≤ 5. So points: sum=2: (1,1); sum=3: (1,2),(2,1); sum=4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1); sum=5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Total points = 1+2+3+4=10. Need 4 lines covering all these points. Exactly k sunny lines. We need to determine possible k.

We can try to see constraints. For n=4, we can possibly construct with k=0 (all non-sunny lines) by using 4 diagonals: x+y=2,3,4,5. That covers all points. So k=0 works.

k=1? Can we have exactly 1 sunny line, 3 non-sunny? Possibly. We need to cover 10 points with 4 lines, one sunny. The non-sunny lines can be horizontals, verticals, diagonals. Could we have a construction? For instance, use the three diagonals x+y=2,3,4 and one sunny line covering the remaining points? But x+y=5 diagonal covers (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) four points. If we use diagonals x+y=2,3,4, that covers points: x+y=2: (1,1); x+y=3: (1,2),(2,1); x+y=4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). So covered 1+2+3=6 points. The remaining points are the four on x+y=5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). We need to cover them with one line (sunny). But is there a line through all four points? Maybe use two diagonals and two horizontals/verticals? Let's try to find a construction with k=1.

General approach: We have n lines. We can choose some lines that cover many points (non-sunny) and a few sunny lines covering the rest. The problem likely asks to determine all k such that there exists some configuration. So we need to find necessary condition on k, and then show that all k that satisfy it are possible.

Observation: The points S are arranged in a triangular lattice. The lines that are not sunny are exactly those parallel to the axes or to the line x+y=0. So they are "axis-aligned" and "diagonal" lines. The set of points S can be partitioned into "diagonals" x+y = s for s=2,...,n+1. Each such diagonal has s-1 points. Also they can be partitioned into vertical lines x = a for a=1,...,n and horizontal lines y = b for b=1,...,n.

Now consider covering S with lines. Each line can be either sunny or not. Non-sunny lines are "axis-aligned" or "diagonal". Sunny lines are any other lines. But note: A sunny line might still contain points that are collinear on a diagonal? Actually a sunny line could intersect a diagonal at a point, but it could also contain multiple points from the same diagonal if it coincides with that diagonal? But it can't coincide because that would be parallel to x+y=0, not sunny. So a sunny line can contain at most one point from each diagonal? Not necessarily: it could contain two points from the same diagonal if the diagonal is line x+y = constant and the sunny line goes through two points on that line; but then the two points are collinear on that line, so the sunny line would have to be that diagonal line itself, which is not sunny. So a sunny line cannot contain two points from the same diagonal (x+y = constant) because that would force it to be that diagonal. Similarly, a sunny line cannot contain two points from the same vertical line x = a, because that would be vertical line itself, not sunny. Nor two points from the same horizontal line y = b, because that would be horizontal. So a sunny line can contain at most one point from each of the lines parallel to axes or diagonals? More precisely, any two points in S that are collinear on a line of slope 0, ∞, or -1 are not allowed to be on a sunny line; but a sunny line could contain two points that are not on such a line. So if a sunny line contains two points, the line through them must have slope not 0,∞,-1. So that is fine. So a sunny line can contain multiple points from S, as long as they are not on those special lines. For example, for n=3, we had a sunny line containing (1,1) and (2,2) which is slope1, that's allowed. That line is y=x, which is not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0. So it's sunny. So indeed a sunny line can contain two points, but we need to see if it can contain more than 2 points from S. For n=4, can a sunny line contain 3 points? Possibly if the three points are collinear with slope not 0,∞,-1. For example, points (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) are on y=x, but (3,3) is not in S for n=4 because sum=6 >5. So not. For n=4, S points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(3,1),(3,2),(4,1). Could there be a line through three of these points? Let's check all triples: The only collinear triples in S are: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1) horizontal y=1; (1,1),(1,2),(1,3) vertical x=1; (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) diagonal x+y=4; (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) diagonal x+y=5 (4 points). Also maybe (1,2),(2,3)? Not collinear with third? Check (1,2),(2,3),(3,4) but (3,4) not in S. So no other triple. So any line through three points from S is one of those, all not sunny. So a sunny line can contain at most 2 points from S. For n=5, S has more points; could there be a line through three points that is not axis-aligned or diagonal? Possibly if points are on a line with slope not 0,∞,-1. For instance, points (1,1),(2,3),(3,5) are collinear with slope 2, but are they in S for n=5? n=5, sum ≤ 6, so (1,1) sum2, (2,3) sum5, (3,5) sum8 >6, not in S. So not. But maybe (1,4),(2,3),(3,2) is diagonal x+y=5; not sunny. (1,2),(2,4),(3,6) sum? (3,6) sum9 >6. So likely for any n, the only collinear triples in S are those on the boundaries: horizontal lines y=1 (points (1,1),(2,1),...,(n,1)), vertical lines x=1 (points (1,1),(1,2),...,(1,n)), and diagonal lines x+y = s for s=3,...,n+1? Actually diagonal x+y = s contains points (1,s-1),(2,s-2),...,(s-1,1). For s from 3 to n+1, there are s-1 points. So for s=3, points (1,2),(2,1) two points; s=4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) three points; s=5: four points; etc. So there are collinear triples for s≥4. So any line through three points from S must be one of these three families. So indeed a sunny line can contain at most 2 points from S. That's an important observation.

Thus each sunny line can cover at most 2 points of S. Non-sunny lines can cover many points (up to n, because a horizontal line y=1 contains n points? Actually for given n, the points with y=1 are (1,1),(2,1),...,(n,1). That's n points. Similarly vertical x=1 contains n points. Diagonal x+y = n+1 contains n points? Actually x+y = n+1 gives points (1,n),(2,n-1),...,(n,1). That's n points. So non-sunny lines can cover up to n points. So for covering all N = n(n+1)/2 points with n lines, if we have many sunny lines, they cover at most 2 each, so the total coverage (counting each point at least once) must be at least N. Let's denote s = number of sunny lines, t = number of non-sunny lines, so s + t = n. The total number of points covered (counting multiplicities) is at most 2s + (max points per non-sunny line) * t. But we only need union coverage. However if we want to minimize the number of points covered by non-sunny lines, we might use them to cover many points. So we need to find necessary condition on s.

Given that we can choose the lines, we want to cover all points. The maximum number of points a line can cover from S is n (for horizontal/vertical/diagonal). But note: there are only 3 lines that can cover n points: y=1, x=1, and x+y = n+1. However we can have multiple non-sunny lines; they can be any lines of those types. For instance, we could use multiple horizontals: y=1, y=2, etc. Each such line covers a set of points with that b. For a given b, the points with that b are (1,b),(2,b),...,(n+1-b,b) because a+b ≤ n+1 => a ≤ n+1-b. So the number of points on horizontal y=b is n+1-b, which is at most n. So a horizontal line can cover up to n points, but for b=1 it covers n points, b=2 covers n-1 points, etc. So the maximum is n.

Similarly vertical x=a covers points (a,1),...,(a,n+1-a) so number n+1-a.

Diagonal x+y = s covers s-1 points.

So we have many potential lines.

Now we need to cover all points. Each point is in some vertical, horizontal, and diagonal. The covering number problem: we have n lines, need to cover N points. Since n lines can cover at most n * n = n^2 points if each line covers n points, but we only need N = n(n+1)/2 ≈ n^2/2. So it's plausible.

But we need to consider exactly k sunny lines. So s = k, t = n - k.

Let's try to find constraints on k.

Suppose we try to minimize the number of sunny lines needed. That is, we want to see if for a given k, we can have a covering with exactly k sunny lines. So we need to see if there exists a configuration with exactly k sunny lines.

Not exactly because non-sunny lines may cover overlapping points. Maybe we can choose a different set of 3 non-sunny lines that yields a different set of missing points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines.

We need to explore systematically. Since n=5, we have 5 lines total. Perhaps there is a known result: For n≥3, the possible k are exactly those where k ≡ n (mod 2) and k ≤ n? Or maybe k can be any nonnegative integer except 2? For n=3, k=0,1,3; for n=4, k=0,1,3; for n=5, we have found k=0,1,? Not yet k=2 or 3. Let's try k=3 for n=5. That would be 3 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines. Need to cover 15 points with 2 non-sunny lines and 3 sunny lines. So we need to see if we can have 2 non-sunny lines covering at least 9 points, and then the remaining points (≤6) can be covered by 3 sunny lines (each covering at most 2). So U_2 ≥9, leaving 15-U_2 ≤6. And we need to cover those remaining points with 3 sunny lines, which is possible if the remaining points can be partitioned into at most 3 pairs (since 3 lines cover at most 6 points, but if less, we can cover each point with a separate line; but we have exactly 3 sunny lines, they can cover up to 6 points, but we might not need all to cover 2 each; we can have some lines covering 1 point each). So we need the set of remaining points to be such that its size ≤6 and can be covered by 3 lines, each sunny. Since any single point can be covered by a sunny line (choose slope not 0,∞,-1), we can always cover any set of points of size ≤3 with 3 sunny lines (each covering one point). For size 4, we could have two lines covering two each, or one covering two and two covering one each. For size 5, we could have two covering two each and one covering one, etc. So the main constraint is that we need to find 2 non-sunny lines that cover a large portion, and then the remaining points can be covered by 3 sunny lines (any set of points can be covered by 3 lines as long as we have enough lines, but we need to ensure that the sunny lines are distinct from each other and from the non-sunny lines, and that they are sunny. For any set of points, we can always take a sunny line through each point individually, provided we have enough lines. But we have exactly 3 sunny lines. So if the remaining set has size up to 3, we can use 3 lines each covering one point. If it has size 4, we need to cover 4 points with 3 lines; we could have one line covering two points (if possible) and the other two covering one each. That requires a sunny pair among the 4 points. If no sunny pair exists, we might still cover them by having some lines cover points that are not in the remaining set? But we need to cover exactly the remaining points, and we can choose the lines arbitrarily. If there is no sunny pair among the 4 points, we cannot cover two of them with a single sunny line; we would need to cover each of the 4 with a separate line, but we only have 3 lines, so one point would be left uncovered. So we need the remaining points to be such that they can be covered by 3 sunny lines. That is, the set of remaining points must be "3-coverable" by sunny lines. Since we can always take a line through a single point, the only problematic case is when the size is >3; then we need to combine at least two points into one line, and that line must be sunny. So we need to find a set of 2 non-sunny lines whose union leaves a set of points of size ≤3, or if size 4, then there must be a sunny pair among them, etc.

Thus for k=2, we needed 2 sunny lines to cover ≤4 points, and if 4 points, we needed a sunny pair. For k=3, we need 3 sunny lines to cover ≤6 points, and if 6 points, we need three sunny pairs (disjoint). But we can also have some lines covering 1 point; the number of lines is 3, so we can cover at most 6 points, but if we have 6 points, we need each line to cover exactly 2 points, and those pairs must be sunny. If we have 5 points, we can have two lines covering 2 each and one covering 1, requiring at least one sunny pair among the 5 points. So it's not too restrictive.

Let's step back. The problem asks: "Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following: for all positive integers a and b with a+b ≤ n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines; and exactly k of the n lines are sunny." So we need to characterize the possible k.

From the small n, it seems that k can be 0,1,3 for n=3,4,5,6. Possibly for n≥3, the only possible k are 0,1, and n? But n=3, k=3 is possible; n=4, k=3; n=5, k=3; n=6, k=3; but also k=0,1. Is k=2 possible for some n? For n=3, no; n=4, no; n=5, no; n=6, we haven't found. Could k=2 be possible for larger n? Let's test n=7. We need to see if we can construct with k=2. The pattern might be that k must be odd? For n=3, possible k=0,1,3 (odd except 0); n=4, 0,1,3; n=5, 0,1,3; n=6, 0,1,3. So k=2 never appears for n up to 6. Could k=2 appear for n=7? Possibly we need to find a general necessary condition.

Let's derive a necessary condition using covering numbers and counting.

We have N = n(n+1)/2 points in S. We have n lines, exactly k sunny. The non-sunny lines (t = n-k) are from families: horizontal, vertical, diagonal (slope -1). Each such line covers a set of points that lie on that line. For a given line of slope 0 (y=b), it covers points with that b. The number of points on that line is n+1-b (since a from 1 to n+1-b). For a vertical line x=a, points count = n+1-a. For a diagonal line x+y=s, points count = s-1 (but note that s ranges from 2 to n+1, so max is n). So the maximum points per non-sunny line is n.

The sunny lines can cover at most 2 points each. So the total number of points covered by the lines, counting multiplicities, is at most 2k + t*n. But the union must be N. So we need 2k + t*n ≥ N. Since t = n-k, this gives 2k + (n-k)n ≥ n(n+1)/2 => 2k + n^2 - kn ≥ n(n+1)/2 => multiply 2: 4k + 2n^2 - 2kn ≥ n(n+1) => 4k + 2n^2 - 2kn - n^2 - n ≥ 0 => 4k + n^2 - n - 2kn ≥ 0 => k(4 - 2n) + n(n-1) ≥ 0 => 2k(n-2) ≤ n(n-1) => k ≤ n(n-1)/(2(n-2)). For n=3, this gives k ≤ 3*2/(2*1)=3; for n=4, k ≤ 4*3/(2*2)=12/4=3; for n=5, k ≤ 5*4/(2*3)=20/6≈3.33, so k ≤3; for n=6, k ≤ 6*5/(2*4)=30/8=3.75, so k ≤3; for n=7, k ≤ 7*6/(2*5)=42/10=4.2, so k ≤4; for n=8, k ≤ 8*7/(2*6)=56/12≈4.667, so k ≤4; for n=9, k ≤ 9*8/(2*7)=72/14≈5.14, so k ≤5; etc. So for n≥7, the bound allows k=4. So possible k could be 0,1,2,3,4,... up to that bound. But we need to see which are actually attainable.

From the examples, we have k=0,1,3 for n=3-6. For n=7, maybe k=0,1,3,4? Let's try to construct k=3 for n=7 using diagonals? That would be like using 4 diagonals? Since we need 7 lines total; if we use 3 non-sunny and 4 sunny? That would be k=4? Actually for k=3, we need 3 sunny and 4 non-sunny. We need to cover all points with 7 lines. It might be possible.

We need to solve a combinatorial geometry problem: Given integer n ≥ 3, determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying:

(1) For all positive integers a, b with a+b ≤ n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines.

(2) Exactly k of the n lines are sunny.

Definition: A line is sunny if it is not parallel to the x-axis, the y-axis, or the line x+y=0.

Thus a line is not sunny if it is parallel to x-axis (horizontal), parallel to y-axis (vertical), or parallel to x+y=0 (i.e., slope -1). So sunny lines are those with slopes not equal to 0, undefined (vertical), or -1. Also note: the line x+y=0 has slope -1. So sunny lines have slopes s ∈ ℝ \ {0, ∞, -1}. But the problem says "not parallel to any of the x-axis, the y-axis, or the line x+y=0". So indeed any line that is horizontal, vertical, or slope -1 is not sunny. All other lines are sunny.

We have n distinct lines, exactly k sunny. So n - k lines are not sunny, i.e., they are either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. But note: distinct lines, so they could be multiple horizontals, multiples verticals, multiples slope -1, but they are all distinct lines.

Condition (1): For all positive integers a, b with a+b ≤ n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines. So we have a finite set of integer lattice points: all points (a,b) with a,b positive integers (positive meaning ≥1) and a+b ≤ n+1. That is a triangular region of points. We need to cover all these points with n lines (distinct). The lines can be any lines in the plane, not necessarily passing through integer points. But they must contain each such point.

We are to determine all possible k (nonnegative integers) for which such a set of lines exists.

We need to find constraints on k given n, and then show that all k satisfying some condition are attainable.

Let's denote S = {(a,b) ∈ ℤ^2 : a≥1, b≥1, a+b ≤ n+1}. Number of points: For each sum s from 2 to n+1, there are s-1 points (since a from 1 to s-1, b = s-a). So total points = Σ_{s=2}^{n+1} (s-1) = Σ_{t=1}^{n} t = n(n+1)/2. That's the number of points.

We have n lines covering these points. Each line can cover many points, but note that points are on integer lattice. However lines can be arbitrary, not necessarily through integer points; but they must pass through each point in S. So each point must lie on some line. The lines are distinct.

We need to find possible k such that there exists a set of n lines with exactly k sunny lines covering all points.

We need to consider the covering properties. This is reminiscent of covering a set of points with lines, like the "no three collinear" or "covering with lines" problems. Here we have many points, and we need exactly n lines. Since there are n(n+1)/2 points, the average number of points per line is (n+1)/2. That is not huge; lines can cover many points if they contain many collinear points.

Observation: The points S lie on integer lattice. Consider the set of points with a+b = constant. They are on lines of slope -1? Actually points with a+b = s lie on the line x+y = s. So each such set is a line segment (the line x+y = s). But we are covering points, not the whole line. So a line that is exactly x+y = constant would cover all points on that diagonal (the integer points). However the line x+y = s is parallel to x+y=0, so it is not sunny (since it's parallel to x+y=0). Indeed any line parallel to x+y=0 has slope -1. So lines with slope -1 are not sunny. So we could use such lines to cover many points.

Similarly, horizontal lines y = constant cover points with same b. Vertical lines x = constant cover points with same a. These are not sunny. So the non-sunny lines are exactly the lines with slope 0, undefined, or -1. So they are "axis-aligned" and "diagonal" lines.

Given that we have exactly k sunny lines, the remaining n-k lines are not sunny. So we can use some horizontals, verticals, and diagonals (slope -1). The condition that the lines are distinct.

Perhaps with k=2, we can use two sunny lines that together cover all points except maybe some that are covered by a non-sunny line. Since we have 3 lines, we can have 2 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line. The non-sunny line could be horizontal, vertical, or diagonal. Let's try to cover all points with 2 sunny lines and 1 diagonal. The diagonal could be x+y=2 covering (1,1). Then we need to cover the other 5 points with two sunny lines. Is it possible that two lines cover 5 points in S? Possibly if each line covers multiple points. But note that the points are in a triangular arrangement. Let's list S for n=3: (1,1), (1,2), (1,3), (2,1), (2,2), (3,1). A line can contain at most 3 points? For example, horizontal y=1 contains (1,1),(2,1),(3,1) three points. Vertical x=1 contains (1,1),(1,2),(1,3) three points. Diagonal x+y=3 contains (1,2),(2,1) two points. Diagonal x+y=4 contains (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) three points. So a line can cover up to 3 points. So two lines could cover at most 6 points. But the diagonal is not sunny, and the horizontals/verticals are not sunny. So any line that covers 3 points from S is not sunny. So for a sunny line, it can cover at most 2 points from S. Because if it covered 3, it would be one of those three lines, which are not sunny. Could it cover exactly 3 points if the three points are collinear but not on those lines? No other triple collinear because any three points from S would be on one of these lines. So indeed any line that contains three points from S is one of those three lines, all not sunny. Now if we have 2 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line, the non-sunny line could cover up to 3 points. However the points covered by the non-sunny line might overlap with points covered by sunny lines, but that's fine as long as all points are covered. But we need to cover all 6 points. The points S: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1). We need three pairs, each pair collinear on a line, and each line must be sunny (slope not 0,∞,-1). Thus for n=3, possible k: 0,1,3. k=2 not possible.

Now n=4. Let's test small n to see pattern. n≥3, so n=4. Points: a,b positive, a+b ≤ 5. So points: sum=2: (1,1); sum=3: (1,2),(2,1); sum=4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1); sum=5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Total points = 1+2+3+4=10. Need 4 lines covering all these points. Exactly k sunny lines. We need to determine possible k.

We can try to see constraints. For n=4, we can possibly construct with k=0 (all non-sunny lines) by using 4 diagonals: x+y=2,3,4,5. That covers all points. So k=0 works.

k=1? Can we have exactly 1 sunny line, 3 non-sunny? Possibly. We need to cover 10 points with 4 lines, one sunny. The non-sunny lines can be horizontals, verticals, diagonals. Could we have a construction? For instance, use the three diagonals x+y=2,3,4 and one sunny line covering the remaining points? But x+y=5 diagonal covers (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) four points. If we use diagonals x+y=2,3,4, that covers points: x+y=2: (1,1); x+y=3: (1,2),(2,1); x+y=4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). So covered 1+2+3=6 points. The remaining points are the four on x+y=5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). We need to cover them with one line (sunny). But is there a line through all four points? They are collinear on x+y=5, which is diagonal slope -1, not sunny. So we cannot use a sunny line covering all four. Could we use a different set of non-sunny lines? Maybe use two diagonals and two horizontals/verticals? Let's try to find a construction with k=1.

General approach: We have n lines. We can choose some lines that cover many points (non-sunny) and a few sunny lines covering the rest. The problem likely asks to determine all k such that there exists some configuration. So we need to find necessary condition on k, and then show that all k that satisfy it are possible.

Observation: The points S are arranged in a triangular lattice. The lines that are not sunny are exactly those parallel to the axes or to the line x+y=0. So they are "axis-aligned" and "diagonal" lines. The set of points S can be partitioned into "diagonals" x+y = s for s=2,...,n+1. Each such diagonal has s-1 points. Also they can be partitioned into vertical lines x = a for a=1,...,n and horizontal lines y = b for b=1,...,n.

Now consider covering S with lines. Each line can be either sunny or not. Non-sunny lines are "axis-aligned" or "diagonal". Sunny lines are any other lines. But note: A sunny line might still contain points that are collinear on a diagonal? Actually a sunny line could intersect a diagonal at a point, but it could also contain multiple points from the same diagonal if it coincides with that diagonal? But it can't coincide because that would be parallel to x+y=0, not sunny. So a sunny line can contain at most one point from each diagonal? Not necessarily: it could contain two points from the same diagonal if the diagonal is line x+y = constant and the sunny line goes through two points on that line; but then the two points are collinear on that line, so the sunny line would have to be that diagonal line itself, which is not sunny. So a sunny line cannot contain two points from the same diagonal (x+y = constant) because that would force it to be that diagonal. Similarly, a sunny line cannot contain two points from the same vertical line x = a, because that would be vertical line itself, not sunny. Nor two points from the same horizontal line y = b, because that would be horizontal. So a sunny line can contain at most one point from each of the lines parallel to axes or diagonals? More precisely, any two points in S that are collinear on a line of slope 0, ∞, or -1 are not allowed to be on a sunny line; but a sunny line could contain two points that are not on such a line. So if a sunny line contains two points, the line through them must have slope not 0,∞,-1. So that is fine. So a sunny line can contain multiple points from S, as long as they are not on those special lines. For example, for n=3, we had a sunny line containing (1,1) and (2,2) which is slope1, that's allowed. That line is y=x, which is not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0. So it's sunny. So indeed a sunny line can contain two points, but we need to see if it can contain more than 2 points from S. For n=4, can a sunny line contain 3 points? Possibly if the three points are collinear with slope not 0,∞,-1. For example, points (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) are on y=x, but (3,3) is not in S for n=4 because sum=6 >5. So not. For n=4, S points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(3,1),(3,2),(4,1). Could there be a line through three of these points? Let's check all triples: The only collinear triples in S are: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1) horizontal y=1; (1,1),(1,2),(1,3) vertical x=1; (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) diagonal x+y=4; (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) diagonal x+y=5 (4 points). Also maybe (1,2),(2,3)? Not collinear with third? Check (1,2),(2,3),(3,4) but (3,4) not in S. So no other triple. So any line through three points from S is one of those, all not sunny. So a sunny line can contain at most 2 points from S. For n=5, S has more points; could there be a line through three points that is not axis-aligned or diagonal? Possibly if points are on a line with slope not 0,∞,-1. For instance, points (1,1),(2,3),(3,5) are collinear with slope 2, but are they in S for n=5? n=5, sum ≤ 6, so (1,1) sum2, (2,3) sum5, (3,5) sum8 >6, not in S. So not. But maybe (1,4),(2,3),(3,2) is diagonal x+y=5; not sunny. (1,2),(2,4),(3,6) sum? (3,6) sum9 >6. So likely for any n, the only collinear triples in S are those on the boundaries: horizontal lines y=1 (points (1,1),(2,1),...,(n,1)), vertical lines x=1 (points (1,1),(1,2),...,(1,n)), and diagonal lines x+y = s for s=3,...,n+1? Actually diagonal x+y = s contains points (1,s-1),(2,s-2),...,(s-1,1). For s from 3 to n+1, there are s-1 points. So for s=3, points (1,2),(2,1) two points; s=4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) three points; s=5: four points; etc. So there are collinear triples for s≥4. So any line through three points from S must be one of these three families. So indeed a sunny line can contain at most 2 points from S. That's an important observation.

Thus each sunny line can cover at most 2 points of S. Non-sunny lines can cover many points (up to n, because a horizontal line y=1 contains n points? Actually for given n, the points with y=1 are (1,1),(2,1),...,(n,1). That's n points. Similarly vertical x=1 contains n points. Diagonal x+y = n+1 contains n points? Actually x+y = n+1 gives points (1,n),(2,n-1),...,(n,1). That's n points. So non-sunny lines can cover up to n points. So for covering all N = n(n+1)/2 points with n lines, if we have many sunny lines, they cover at most 2 each, so the total coverage (counting each point at least once) must be at least N. Let's denote s = number of sunny lines, t = number of non-sunny lines, so s + t = n. The total number of points covered (counting multiplicities) is at most 2s + (max points per non-sunny line) * t. But we only need union coverage. However if we want to minimize the number of points covered by non-sunny lines, we might use them to cover many points. So we need to find necessary condition on s.

Given that we can choose the lines, we want to cover all points. The maximum number of points a line can cover from S is n (for horizontal/vertical/diagonal). But note: there are only 3 lines that can cover n points: y=1, x=1, and x+y = n+1. However we can have multiple non-sunny lines; they can be any lines of those types. For instance, we could use multiple horizontals: y=1, y=2, etc. Each such line covers a set of points with that b. For a given b, the points with that b are (1,b),(2,b),...,(n+1-b,b) because a+b ≤ n+1 => a ≤ n+1-b. So the number of points on horizontal y=b is n+1-b, which is at most n. So a horizontal line can cover up to n points, but for b=1 it covers n points, b=2 covers n-1 points, etc. So the maximum is n.

Similarly vertical x=a covers points (a,1),...,(a,n+1-a) so number n+1-a.

Diagonal x+y = s covers s-1 points.

So we have many potential lines.

Now we need to cover all points. Each point is in some vertical, horizontal, and diagonal. The covering number problem: we have n lines, need to cover N points. Since n lines can cover at most n * n = n^2 points if each line covers n points, but we only need N = n(n+1)/2 ≈ n^2/2. So it's plausible.

But we need to consider exactly k sunny lines. So s = k, t = n - k.

Let's try to find constraints on k.

Suppose we try to minimize the number of sunny lines needed. That is, we want to see if for a given k, we can have a covering with exactly k sunny lines. So we need to see if there exists a configuration with exactly k sunny lines.

From n=3, possible k: 0,1,3. For n=4, let's try to see if k=2 possible? We need to cover 10 points with 4 lines, exactly 2 sunny. So 2 non-sunny. Could we cover all points with 2 non-sunny lines and 2 sunny lines? Possibly if the non-sunny lines cover many points. For example, use horizontal y=1 covering (1,1)-(4,1) 4 points; vertical x=1 covering (1,1)-(1,4) 4 points; but they overlap at (1,1). So total points covered by these two lines: 4+4-1=7. Then we need to cover the remaining 3 points: (2,2),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1?) Wait we need to list all points for n=4: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(3,1),(3,2),(4,1). After using y=1 (covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1)) and x=1 (covers (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4)), the uncovered points are: (2,2),(2,3),(3,2). That's 3 points. We have 2 sunny lines to cover them. Could each sunny line cover 2 points? Possibly if we can find two lines each through two of these points, and they are sunny. But note: (2,2) and (2,3) are vertical x=2, not sunny. (2,2) and (3,2) are horizontal y=2, not sunny. (2,3) and (3,2) are slope (2-3)/(3-2) = -1, not sunny. So any pair among these three points yields a non-sunny line. So a sunny line can contain at most one of these points. So with 2 sunny lines, we can cover at most 2 points, leaving one uncovered. So k=2 not possible with that choice. Could we choose different non-sunny lines? Maybe use a diagonal and a horizontal? For instance, use x+y=4 (covers (1,3),(2,2),(3,1)) and x+y=5 (covers (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1)). That's 2 diagonals covering all points except (1,1),(1,2),(2,1). Actually x+y=2 covers (1,1); x+y=3 covers (1,2),(2,1). So if we use x+y=4 and x+y=5, we cover points: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). That's 7 points. Remaining: (1,1),(1,2),(2,1). That's 3 points. Can we cover them with 2 sunny lines? Again any line through two of these points: (1,1)-(1,2) vertical x=1 not sunny; (1,1)-(2,1) horizontal y=1 not sunny; (1,2)-(2,1) slope -1 not sunny. So again each sunny line at most one point. So not enough.

Could we use one horizontal and one diagonal? e.g., y=1 (covers 4 points) and x+y=5 (covers 4 points). Their intersection? (4,1) is on both? y=1 covers (4,1); x+y=5 covers (4,1). So overlap 1. We can try to construct such a configuration.

Thus with that particular choice, k=2 fails.

Maybe we can choose a different set of 3 non-sunny lines that cover a different set of points, perhaps covering more points, leaving fewer uncovered points, and possibly the uncovered points can be covered by 2 sunny lines.

We need U≥11, and the uncovered points set (15-U) must be coverable by 2 sunny lines. Since each sunny line can cover at most 2 points, we need uncovered points ≤4. But also if uncovered points are 4, we need to cover them with 2 sunny lines, each covering 2, which requires that the uncovered points can be partitioned into two pairs, each pair collinear on a sunny line. If uncovered points are 3, we need one line covering 2 and one covering 1, which requires a sunny line through two of them (since the line through the third alone is always possible, but then we need to check that the line through the third is distinct and sunny; any line through a single point can be made sunny by choosing slope not 0,∞,-1). However we also need the sunny lines to be distinct from the non-sunny lines and from each other. So if uncovered points are 3, we need to find a sunny line through two of them, and a sunny line through the third (distinct). But as we saw, no two of the points we have (the missing points from the previous configuration) yield a sunny line. However maybe with a different set of non-sunny lines, the uncovered points might be different, and could contain a pair that is sunny. So we need to search for a set of 3 non-sunny lines such that the uncovered points (the points not on any of those lines) have size ≤4 and can be covered by 2 sunny lines.

Let's systematically consider the possible 3 non-sunny lines. They could be a combination of horizontals, verticals, and diagonals. Since the total points is 15, we need the union of the 3 lines to be at least 11. The maximum possible union size is 15, but we need to see if we can get 11 or 12. The previous configuration gave 12. Could we get 13? Possibly if the lines are chosen to cover many points and have minimal overlap. For 3 lines, the maximum union size is when they are disjoint and each covers many points. But they cannot be completely disjoint because the families have limited overlap? Actually horizontals are disjoint (different b), verticals are disjoint (different a), diagonals are disjoint (different s). However a horizontal and a vertical intersect at a point (if that point is in S). So they are not disjoint. So the union size is sum of sizes minus overlaps. To maximize union, we want lines that cover many points but have small overlap. Overlap occurs at points that lie on two or three lines. Since we have only 3 lines, overlaps are at most 3 points. So the union size is at most sum of sizes minus sum of pairwise overlaps (if no triple overlap). The maximum sum of sizes is 5+5+5=15, but if we take three lines that are all "large", they might overlap at many points. For example, y=1, x=1, and x+y=6: sum sizes=15, overlaps: y=1 ∩ x=1 = (1,1); y=1 ∩ x+y=6 = (5,1); x=1 ∩ x+y=6 = (1,5); so 3 overlaps, union=12. Could we choose lines that are larger but with less overlap? For instance, use two diagonals and one horizontal? Diagonals: s=6 (5 points) and s=5 (4 points) are disjoint, union=9. Then add horizontal y=1 (5 points). Overlaps: y=1 ∩ s=6: (5,1) (since s=6 includes (5,1)); y=1 ∩ s=5: (4,1) (since s=5 includes (4,1)); s=5 ∩ s=6: none. So overlaps = 2, union = 5+4+5 - 2 = 12. Still 12. Add vertical x=1 instead? s=6 (5), s=5 (4), x=1 (5). So still 12.

What about using three diagonals? s=6 (5), s=5 (4), s=4 (3) total 12, no overlap because different sums, so union=12. So U=12.

What about using one horizontal, one vertical, and one diagonal? We already did y=1,x=1,s=6 gave 12. Maybe we can choose a different set of 3 non-sunny lines that yields a different set of missing points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines.

We need to explore systematically. Since n=5, we have 5 lines total. Perhaps there is a known result: For n≥3, the possible k are exactly those where k ≡ n (mod 2) and k ≤ n? Or maybe k can be any nonnegative integer except 2? For n=3, k=0,1,3; for n=4, k=0,1,3; for n=5, we have found k=0,1,? Not yet k=2 or 3. Let's try k=3 for n=5. That would be 3 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines. Need to cover 15 points with 2 non-sunny lines and 3 sunny lines. The non-sunny lines can cover at most 2*5=10 points, so the sunny lines must cover the remaining at least 5 points. Since each sunny line covers at most 2, total sunny coverage ≤6, so it's possible if the non-sunny lines cover 9 or 10 points, leaving 6 or 5 points for sunny lines. So we need to see if we can have 2 non-sunny lines covering at least 9 points, and then the remaining points (≤6) can be covered by 3 sunny lines (each covering at most 2). So U_2 ≥9, leaving 15-U_2 ≤6. And we need to cover those remaining points with 3 sunny lines, which is possible if the remaining points can be partitioned into at most 3 pairs (since 3 lines cover at most 6 points, but if less, we can cover each point with a separate line; but we have exactly 3 sunny lines, they can cover up to 6 points, but we might not need all to cover 2 each; we can have some lines covering 1 point each). So we need the set of remaining points to be such that its size ≤6 and can be covered by 3 lines, each sunny. Since any single point can be covered by a sunny line (choose slope not 0,∞,-1), we can always cover any set of points of size ≤3 with 3 sunny lines (each covering one point). For size 4, we could have two lines covering two each, or one covering two and two covering one each. For size 5, we could have two covering two each and one covering one, etc. So the main constraint is that we need to find 2 non-sunny lines that cover a large portion, and then the remaining points can be covered by 3 sunny lines (any set of points can be covered by 3 lines as long as we have enough lines, but we need to ensure that the sunny lines are distinct from each other and from the non-sunny lines, and that they are sunny. For any set of points, we can always take a sunny line through each point individually, provided we have enough lines. But we have exactly 3 sunny lines. So if the remaining set has size up to 3, we can use 3 lines each covering one point. If it has size 4, we need to cover 4 points with 3 lines; we could have one line covering two points (if possible) and the other two covering one each. That requires a sunny pair among the 4 points. If no sunny pair exists, we might still cover them by having some lines cover points that are not in the remaining set? But we need to cover exactly the remaining points, and we can choose the lines arbitrarily. If there is no sunny pair among the 4 points, we cannot cover two of them with a single sunny line; we would need to cover each of the 4 with a separate line, but we only have 3 lines, so one point would be left uncovered. So we need the remaining points to be such that they can be covered by 3 sunny lines. That is, the set of remaining points must be "3-coverable" by sunny lines. Since we can always take a line through a single point, the only problematic case is when the size is >3; then we need to combine at least two points into one line, and that line must be sunny. So we need to find a set of 2 non-sunny lines whose union leaves a set of points of size ≤3, or if size 4, then there must be a sunny pair among them, etc.

Thus for k=2, we needed 2 sunny lines to cover ≤4 points, and if 4 points, we needed a sunny pair. For k=3, we need 3 sunny lines to cover ≤6 points, and if 6 points, we need three sunny pairs (disjoint). But we can also have some lines covering 1 point; the number of lines is 3, so we can cover at most 6 points, but if we have 6 points, we need each line to cover exactly 2 points, and those pairs must be sunny. If we have 5 points, we can have two lines covering 2 each and one covering 1, requiring at least one sunny pair among the 5 points. So it's not too restrictive.

Given that we already have a construction for k=1 (3 non-sunny lines covering 14 points, leaving 1 point for sunny line). For k=2, we need 3 non-sunny lines covering at least 11 points, leaving ≤4 points. We tried one set (y=1,x=1,s=6) left 3 points, but those 3 points had no sunny pair. Could we choose a different set of 3 non-sunny lines that covers a different set of points, leaving maybe 2 points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines (each covering one point) or 4 points that contain a sunny pair.

Let's try to find 3 non-sunny lines that cover as many points as possible, and then examine the uncovered set.

We can consider all possible combinations of three lines from the families. But perhaps there is a more systematic approach: The points S form a triangular grid. Thus it seems that for n=6, any union of 4 non-sunny lines covers at most 18 points, leaving at least 3 points. And those 3 points are often sets that do not contain a sunny pair. Could there be a set of 4 lines that covers 19 points? As argued, the maximum union of 4 lines is 18, because the complement of the union of the three largest lines is 6 points, and any fourth line can cover at most 3 of them, so union ≤ 15+3=18. But is it possible that the three largest lines are not the three we considered? Maybe there is a different set of three lines that covers more than 15 points? Let's examine all possible 3-line combinations. The maximum union of 3 lines from the families is indeed 15. Could it be 16? Suppose we take y=1 (6), y=2 (5), and y=3 (4) gives 15, disjoint. If we take y=1, y=2, and x=1, we got 15 as well. If we take y=1, x=1, and s=7, we got 15. The fourth line can be any line. The points not covered by the three lines are 6 points. The fourth line can contain at most 6 of them, but it must be a line from families. Thus for n=6, no combination yields a 4-point uncovered set that is matchable into two sunny pairs. It seems plausible that for n=6, k=2 is impossible. However we haven't considered the possibility that the uncovered set could be of size 4 but not necessarily coming from a 3-line union plus one line; maybe we could have 4 non-sunny lines that are not all "large" but some combination that yields union size 17 and uncovered set of 4 points that is matchable. We can try to find a different set of 4 lines that might cover 17 points and leave a matchable set. Let's step back. The problem asks: "Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following: for all positive integers a and b with a+b ≤ n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines; and exactly k of the n lines are sunny." So we need to characterize the possible k.

From the small n, it seems that k can be 0,1,3 for n=3,4,5,6. Possibly for n≥3, the only possible k are 0,1, and n? But n=3, k=3 is possible; n=4, k=3; n=5, k=3; n=6, k=3; but also k=0,1. Is k=2 possible for some n? For n=3, no; n=4, no; n=5, no; n=6, we haven't found. Could k=2 be possible for larger n? Let's test n=7. We need to see if we can construct with k=2. The pattern might be that k must be odd? For n=3, possible k=0,1,3 (odd except 0); n=4, 0,1,3; n=5, 0,1,3; n=6, 0,1,3. So k=2 never appears for n up to 6. Could k=2 appear for n=7? Possibly we need to find a general necessary condition.

Let's derive a necessary condition using covering numbers and counting.

We have N = n(n+1)/2 points in S. We have n lines, exactly k sunny. The non-sunny lines (t = n-k) are from families: horizontal, vertical, diagonal (slope -1). Each such line covers a set of points that lie on that line. For a given line of slope 0 (y=b), it covers points with that b. The number of points on that line is n+1-b (since a from 1 to n+1-b). For a vertical line x=a, points count = n+1-a. For a diagonal line x+y=s, points count = s-1 (but note that s ranges from 2 to n+1, so max is n). So the maximum points per non-sunny line is n.

The sunny lines can cover at most 2 points each. So the total number of points covered by the lines, counting multiplicities, is at most 2k + t*n. But the union must be N. So we need 2k + t*n ≥ N. Since t = n-k, this gives 2k + (n-k)n ≥ n(n+1)/2 => 2k + n^2 - kn ≥ n(n+1)/2 => multiply 2: 4k + 2n^2 - 2kn ≥ n(n+1) => 4k + 2n^2 - 2kn - n^2 - n ≥ 0 => 4k + n^2 - n - 2kn ≥ 0 => k(4 - 2n) + n(n-1) ≥ 0 => 2k(n-2) ≤ n(n-1) => k ≤ n(n-1)/(2(n-2)). For n=3, this gives k ≤ 3*2/(2*1)=3; for n=4, k ≤ 4*3/(2*2)=12/4=3; for n=5, k ≤ 5*4/(2*3)=20/6≈3.33, so k ≤3; for n=6, k ≤ 6*5/(2*4)=30/8=3.75, so k ≤3; for n=7, k ≤ 7*6/(2*5)=42/10=4.2, so k ≤4; for n=8, k ≤ 8*7/(2*6)=56/12≈4.667, so k ≤4; for n=9, k ≤ 9*8/(2*7)=72/14≈5.14, so k ≤5; etc. So for n≥7, the bound allows k=4. So possible k could be 0,1,2,3,4,... up to that bound. But we need to see which are actually attainable.

From the examples, we have k=0,1,3 for n=3-6. For n=7, maybe k=0,1,3,4? Let's try to construct k=3 for n=7 using diagonals? That would be like using 4 diagonals? Since we need 7 lines total; if we use 3 non-sunny and 4 sunny? That would be k=4? Actually for k=3, we need 3 sunny and 4 non-sunny. We need to cover all points with 7 lines. It might be possible.

But we need to determine all k for which there exists some configuration. This is a combinatorial geometry problem likely from an olympiad, and the answer is probably something like: all k such that k is not equal to 2 and k ≤ n? But for n=7, k=2 might be possible? We need to test.

Let's try to see if we can find a configuration for n=7 with k=2. That would be 2 sunny lines and 5 non-sunny lines. Sum of sizes: sunny max 2 each, non-sunny max 7 each (since n=7, horizontals cover up to 7 points). So sum ≤ 4 + 5*7 = 39. N = 28. So it's possible. We need to see if we can choose the lines such that the union covers all 28 points.

We can try to generalize the construction we had for n=6 with k=1. For n=6, k=1 used y=1, x=1, and diagonals s=5,6,7 (that's 5 lines) covering 20 points, leaving 1 point (2,2) for sunny line. Did we have a mistake? For n=6, we have 6 lines total. We had a construction with 3 sunny and 3 non-sunny? Let's re-derive: For n=6, we need 6 lines. We have the triple of diagonals s=2,3,4?
